processing","slug":"natural-language-processing","taxonomy":"post_tag","_links":{"self":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags\/145707"}],"collection":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags"}],"about":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/taxonomies\/post_tag"}],"wp:post_type":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/posts?tags=145707"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/battlefield-companies?tags=145707"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc-media-gallery?tags=145707"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc_video?tags=145707"}],"curies":[{"name":"wp","href":"https:\/\/api.w.org\/{rel}","templated":true}]}},{"id":47433,"link":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/tag\/nlp\/","name":"nlp","slug":"nlp","taxonomy":"post_tag","_links":{"self":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags\/47433"}],"collection":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags"}],"about":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/taxonomies\/post_tag"}],"wp:post_type":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/posts?tags=47433"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/battlefield-companies?tags=47433"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc-media-gallery?tags=47433"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc_video?tags=47433"}],"curies":[{"name":"wp","href":"https:\/\/api.w.org\/{rel}","templated":true}]}},{"id":421223872,"link":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/tag\/openai\/","name":"OpenAI","slug":"openai","taxonomy":"post_tag","_links":{"self":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags\/421223872"}],"collection":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags"}],"about":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/taxonomies\/post_tag"}],"wp:post_type":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/posts?tags=421223872"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/battlefield-companies?tags=421223872"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc-media-gallery?tags=421223872"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc_video?tags=421223872"}],"curies":[{"name":"wp","href":"https:\/\/api.w.org\/{rel}","templated":true}]}}],[],[],[],[]]}},{"id":2261213,"date":"2022-01-24T12:06:57","date_gmt":"2022-01-24T20:06:57","guid":{"rendered":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/?p=2261213"},"modified":"2022-01-24T14:45:09","modified_gmt":"2022-01-24T22:45:09","slug":"nasa-celebrates-private-sector-deployments-of-space-born-tech-in-its-latest-spinoff","status":"publish","type":"post","link":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/01\/24\/nasa-celebrates-private-sector-deployments-of-space-born-tech-in-its-latest-spinoff\/","title":{"rendered":"NASA celebrates private sector deployments of space-born tech in its latest Spinoff"},"content":{"rendered":"<p id=\"speakable-summary\">NASA&#8217;s Spinoff magazine is one of the things I look forward to reading every year. The space agency&#8217;s research trickles down to the rest of the world in surprising and interesting ways, which it tracks and collects in this annual publication. This year is no different, and NASA tech can be found in everything from hiking gadgets to heavy industry and, funnily enough, space.<\/p>\n<p>There are dozens of technologies that have made their way to everyday use in a variety of places <a href=\"https:\/\/spinoff.nasa.gov\/Spinoff-2022-release\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">highlighted in this year&#8217;s issue<\/a>, which you can <a href=\"https:\/\/spinoff.nasa.gov\/sites\/default\/files\/2022-01\/Spinoff.2022.pdf\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">browse here<\/a>. (It&#8217;s about 60 pages, so pour some coffee and settle in.)<\/p>\n<p>I talked with Daniel Lockney, the head of NASA&#8217;s Tech Transfer Program overseeing the deployment of its tech and research among terrestrial companies looking to put it to good use.<\/p><div id=\"tc-target-mid-article-2261213\" class=\"tc-target-mid-article ad-unit--size-auto\"><\/div>\n<p>&#8220;Typically what happens is: NASA develops something, they report it to my office, and we look at it to figure out, first, does it work? And second, who else can use it? And if someone can, we figure out how to get it to them,&#8221; Lockney explained. &#8220;I try to give as much away for free as I can. I&#8217;ve got no direction to generate revenue or bring something back to the U.S. Treasury. The 1958 NASA act that created us says to disseminate our work \u2014 nothing in there about making a dime.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p>The result is cheap or free licensing of interesting tech like compact, long-lasting water filters, unusual mechanical components and other tech that was needed for space or launch purposes but might find a second use on the ground.<\/p>\n<p>Lockney highlighted a couple items in the latest batch that he thought were especially interesting.<\/p>\n<p>&#8220;There was a partnership with GM to develop the Robo-Glove, a functional glove that astronauts will wear to help reduce strain during repetitive tasks and increase grip strength,&#8221; he said. &#8220;Squeezing something on a spacewalk, you can do it a couple times, but if you&#8217;re gripping a tool for the whole afternoon&#8230; so we developed this glove to assist in that work, and now it&#8217;s being used at factories around the world.&#8221;<\/p><div class=\"piano-inline-promo\"><\/div>\n<p><div id=\"attachment_2261392\" style=\"width: 1034px\" class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/bioservo.jpg\"><img aria-describedby=\"caption-attachment-2261392\" loading=\"lazy\" class=\"size-full wp-image-2261392\" src=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/bioservo.jpg\" alt=\"Two images of people wearing a robotic glove at work.\" width=\"1024\" height=\"502\" srcset=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/bioservo.jpg 1249w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/bioservo.jpg?resize=150,73 150w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/bioservo.jpg?resize=300,147 300w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/bioservo.jpg?resize=768,376 768w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/bioservo.jpg?resize=680,333 680w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/bioservo.jpg?resize=50,24 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px\" \/><\/a><p id=\"caption-attachment-2261392\" class=\"wp-caption-text\"><strong>Image Credits:<\/strong> Bioservo Technologies<\/p><\/div><\/p>\n<p>The Swiss company Bioservo licensed the NASA patents for Robo-Glove and has been iterating on the concept for years, and the latest version of its Ironhand device came out last summer. Its most common use case is by employees with hand injuries that might cause them to lose work, but can use the glove to return to the job faster, as well as reduce pain medication.<\/p>\n<p>It&#8217;s not always just a single company licensing a tech. Lockney noted how NASA was the first organization to look into the question of precision agriculture under totally artificial conditions.<\/p>\n<p>&#8220;NASA has a lot of experiments for keeping crews healthy on long distance spaceflight. One thing we have to do is grow our own food, plus there&#8217;s the psychological benefits of seeing plants,&#8221; he said. &#8220;But we needed to find ways to grow crops without a heavy growing medium like soil, or even hydroponics \u2014 water is so heavy, and so valuable. And you have to get the lighting right, but you can&#8217;t use too much energy. So we made these farming techniques to grow a lot of plants in a small space. If you control the plants&#8217; stress, you can really dial in precision growth conditions and improve yield; we actually use a nutritive film that covers the roots, LEDs to give the right spectrum of light and of course there&#8217;s sensors everywhere.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p>&#8220;It&#8217;s a similar situation in cities, how do you provide food to this population without the resource waste of farmland? But we led this research because no one else had the need for it \u2014 it ended up being the direct result of the demands of spaceflight. And now there are a handful of companies doing vertical farms in dense urban areas, actually providing grocery stores with vegetables,&#8221; he continued.<\/p>\n<p>We&#8217;ve actually covered a few, and they&#8217;re still in early days, but the appetite is clearly there for both consumers and investors to have food grown efficiently and within a few blocks of them rather than shipped a thousand miles overseas.<\/p>\n<div class=\"embed breakout\">\n<blockquote class=\"wp-embedded-content\" data-secret=\"lnx1Ypnt8p\"><p><a href=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/10\/10\/on-ten-years-of-the-vertical-farm\/\">On 10 years of &#8216;The Vertical Farm&#8217;<\/a><\/p><\/blockquote>\n<p><iframe class=\"wp-embedded-content\" sandbox=\"allow-scripts\" security=\"restricted\" style=\"position: absolute; clip: rect(1px, 1px, 1px, 1px);\" title=\"&#8220;On 10 years of &#8216;The Vertical Farm&#8217;&#8221; &#8212; TechCrunch\" src=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/10\/10\/on-ten-years-of-the-vertical-farm\/embed\/#?secret=lnx1Ypnt8p\" data-secret=\"lnx1Ypnt8p\" width=\"800\" height=\"450\" frameborder=\"0\" marginwidth=\"0\" marginheight=\"0\" scrolling=\"no\"><\/iframe><\/div>\n<p>NASA work makes its way to leisure as well as life-sustaining industries. At least three of the items in this year&#8217;s Spinoff have to do with outdoors activities like hiking and camping. One, a thin-film radiant barrier originally used to line spacecraft, has made its way to jackets by <a href=\"https:\/\/13-one.com\/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">13-One<\/a> and others as a super-light insulating layer. Aerogel research from the &#8217;90s has made its way to new gear from Seattle-based Outdoor Research (a brand I covet when I walk by their store). And a material called NanoCeram is used in a new <a href=\"https:\/\/nkdlife.com\/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">portable water filter bottle<\/a>.<\/p>\n<p>One new application you wouldn&#8217;t expect to find in a publication about spinoff techs is <a href=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/04\/13\/spacexs-falcon-heavy-rocket-to-deliver-an-astrobotic-lander-and-nasa-water-hunting-rover-to-the-moon-in-2023\/\">Astrobotic&#8217;s Peregrine moon lander<\/a>. In the past such things have been exclusively the domain of nationally backed programs, but with the commercial space sector expanding quickly, NASA tech is valuable to spacefaring companies as well.<\/p>\n<p>Not all of these are new \u2014 some are decades old and still finding new applications or companies to sell them.<\/p>\n<p>&#8220;By the time we&#8217;ve done all the work and we&#8217;ve found a partner doing the commercial stuff, manufacturing, marketing&#8230;. next thing you know, 10 years have passed,&#8221; Lockney said. &#8220;The R&amp;D timeline is long and the commercialization timeline is long.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p>But that means there&#8217;s always something fresh coming out even when the papers or materials are years old. There are dozens more techs and companies worth looking at in this year&#8217;s Spinoff, so take a look. And if you&#8217;ve got time, <a href=\"https:\/\/spinoff.nasa.gov\/spinoff\/archives\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">head to the archives<\/a>.<\/p>\n","protected":false},"excerpt":{"rendered":"<p>NASA&#8217;s Spinoff magazine is one of the things I look forward to reading every year. The space agency&#8217;s research trickles down to the rest of the world in surprising and interesting ways, which it tracks and collects in this annual publication. This year is no different, and NASA tech can be found in everything from Cuban on his online pharmacy: &#8216;Our KPI is how much we can reduce the stress of our patients&#8217;"},"content":{"rendered":"<p id=\"speakable-summary\">Mark Cuban&#8217;s announcement over the weekend of an online pharmacy selling over a hundred generic drugs at near cost was totally unexpected but will likely be welcomed by millions who struggle to afford medication. The billionaire told TechCrunch that the business model is refreshingly simple: &#8220;Lower pricing reduces patient stress, and that will lead to more customers.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p><a href=\"https:\/\/costplusdrugs.com\">The Cost Plus Drug Company<\/a> aims very simply to provide as many common medications as possible in generic form at as low a price as possible. All cash, no IP deals, no insurance companies \u2014 just buy pills for what they cost to make, plus 15 percent to cover overhead.<\/p>\n<p>Asked about ROI, Cuban admitted there isn&#8217;t much to speak of, by design.<\/p><div id=\"tc-target-mid-article-2261311\" class=\"tc-target-mid-article ad-unit--size-auto\"><\/div>\n<p>&#8220;I want to be above break even while maximizing the number of people who can afford their medications,&#8221; he said. &#8220;Shoot. I would be happy if we can make a little, but push pricing of generics sold elsewhere down significantly.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p>&#8220;Our challenge is to keep pushing prices lower,&#8221; not compete with anyone, he continued. &#8220;Our KPI is how much we can reduce the stress of our patients who buy generic meds. When people save a lot of money on their medications, they often will tell others they know that have the same challenges. That word of mouth impacts our growth the most.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p>The company currently offers generic versions of medications <a href=\"https:\/\/costplusdrugs.com\/medications\/\">for everything from migraines to HIV to birth control<\/a>, but there&#8217;s no particular priority to the ones on the list except that they can and should be offered cheaper, explained Cuban. There&#8217;s no board deciding which conditions are next on the list or anything like that.<\/p>\n<p>&#8220;As far as the process, we choose drugs that we can offer at a price that is lower than what is out there already,&#8221; he said simply. &#8220;It&#8217;s like any business.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p>The ultra-straightforward business plan of undercutting middle men and offering a proven product for a better price than anyone else seems almost quaint today, but Cuban knows what he&#8217;s doing \u2014 in a generic way, at least. Asked what advice he had for any startups looking to get into the online pharmacy space, he offered a virtual shrug: &#8220;I don&#8217;t have any. I&#8217;m still learning.&#8221;<\/p>\n","protected":false},"excerpt":{"rendered":"<p>Mark Cuban&#8217;s announcement over the weekend of an online pharmacy selling over a hundred generic drugs at near cost was totally unexpected but will likely be welcomed by millions who struggle to afford medication. The billionaire told TechCrunch that the business model is refreshingly simple: &#8220;Lower pricing reduces patient stress, and that will lead to ?categories=20429"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc-media-gallery?categories=20429"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc_video?categories=20429"}],"curies":[{"name":"wp","href":"https:\/\/api.w.org\/{rel}","templated":true}]}}],[{"id":163934,"link":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/tag\/mark-cuban\/","name":"mark cuban","slug":"mark-cuban","taxonomy":"post_tag","_links":{"self":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags\/163934"}],"collection":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags"}],"about":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/taxonomies\/post_tag"}],"wp:post_type":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/posts?tags=163934"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/battlefield-companies?tags=163934"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc-media-gallery?tags=163934"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc_video?tags=163934"}],"curies":[{"name":"wp","href":"https:\/\/api.w.org\/{rel}","templated":true}]}},{"id":154753,"link":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/tag\/online-pharmacy\/","name":"online pharmacy","slug":"online-pharmacy","taxonomy":"post_tag","_links":{"self":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags\/154753"}],"collection":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags"}],"about":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/taxonomies\/post_tag"}],"wp:post_type":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/posts?tags=154753"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/battlefield-companies?tags=154753"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc-media-gallery?tags=154753"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc_video?tags=154753"}],"curies":[{"name":"wp","href":"https:\/\/api.w.org\/{rel}","templated":true}]}}],[],[],[],[]]}},{"id":2260829,"date":"2022-01-24T09:32:13","date_gmt":"2022-01-24T17:32:13","guid":{"rendered":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/?p=2260829"},"modified":"2022-01-24T12:30:19","modified_gmt":"2022-01-24T20:30:19","slug":"meta-leaps-into-the-supercomputer-game-with-its-ai-research-supercluster","status":"publish","type":"post","link":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/01\/24\/meta-leaps-into-the-supercomputer-game-with-its-ai-research-supercluster\/","title":{"rendered":"Meta leaps into the supercomputer game with its AI Research SuperCluster"},"content":{"rendered":"<p id=\"speakable-summary\">There&#8217;s a global competition to build the biggest, most powerful computers on the planet, and Meta (AKA Facebook) is about to jump into the melee with the <a href=\"https:\/\/ai.facebook.com\/blog\/ai-rsc\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">&#8220;AI Research SuperCluster,&#8221; or RSC<\/a>. Once fully operational, it may well sit in the top 10 fastest supercomputers in the world, which it will use for the massive number crunching needed for language and computer vision modeling.<\/p>\n<p>Large AI models, of which OpenAI&#8217;s GPT-3 is probably the best known, don&#8217;t get put together on laptops and desktops; they&#8217;re the final product of weeks and months of sustained calculations by high-performance computing systems that dwarf even the most cutting-edge gaming rig. And the faster you can complete the training process for a model, the faster you can test it and produce a new and better one. When training times are measured in months, that really matters.<\/p>\n<p>RSC is up and running and the company&#8217;s researchers are already putting it to work&#8230; with user-generated data, it must be said, though Meta was careful to say that it is encrypted until training time and the whole facility is isolated from the wider internet.<\/p><div id=\"tc-target-mid-article-2260829\" class=\"tc-target-mid-article ad-unit--size-auto\"><\/div>\n<p>The team that put RSC together is rightly proud at having pulled this off almost entirely remotely \u2014 supercomputers are surprisingly physical constructions, with base considerations like heat, cabling and interconnect affecting performance and design. Exabytes of storage sound big enough digitally, but they actually need to exist somewhere too, on site and accessible at a microsecond&#8217;s notice. (Pure Storage is <a href=\"https:\/\/www.purestorage.com\/company\/newsroom\/press-releases\/pure-partners-with-meta-on-ai-research-supercluster.html\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">also proud of the setup they put together for this<\/a>.)<\/p>\n<p>RSC is currently 760 Nvidia DGX A100 systems with a total 6,080 GPUs, which Meta claims should put it approximately in competition with Perlmutter at Lawrence Berkeley National Lab. That&#8217;s the fifth most powerful supercomputer in operation right now, <a href=\"https:\/\/www.top500.org\/lists\/top500\/2021\/11\/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">according to longtime ranking site Top 500<\/a>. (No. 1 is <a href=\"https:\/\/www.r-ccs.riken.jp\/en\/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Fugaku<\/a> in Japan by a long shot, in case you&#8217;re wondering.)<\/p>\n<p>That could change as the company continues building out the system. Ultimately they plan for it to be about three times more powerful, which would in theory put it in the running for third place.<\/p>\n<p><span class=\"embed-youtube embed breakout embed--video\" style=\"text-align:center; display: block;\"><iframe class=\"youtube-player\" width=\"640\" height=\"360\" src=\"https:\/\/www.youtube.com\/embed\/fZnykn1tDSE?version=3&#038;rel=1&#038;showsearch=0&#038;showinfo=1&#038;iv_load_policy=1&#038;fs=1&#038;hl=en-US&#038;autohide=2&#038;wmode=transparent\" allowfullscreen=\"true\" style=\"border:0;\" sandbox=\"allow-scripts allow-same-origin allow-popups allow-presentation\"><\/iframe><\/span><\/p>\n<p>There&#8217;s arguably a caveat in there. Systems like second-place Summit at Lawrence Livermore National Lab are employed for research purposes, where precision is at a premium. If you&#8217;re simulating the molecules in a region of the Earth&#8217;s atmosphere at unprecedented detail levels, you need to take every calculation out to a whole lot of decimal points. And that means those calculations are more computationally expensive.<\/p>\n<p>Meta explained that AI applications don&#8217;t require a similar degree of precision, since the results don&#8217;t hinge on that thousandth of a percent \u2014 inference operations end up producing things like &#8220;90% certainty this is a cat,&#8221; and if that number were 89% or 91% wouldn&#8217;t make a big difference. The difficulty is more about achieving 90% certainty for a million objects or phrases rather than a hundred.<\/p>\n<p>It&#8217;s an oversimplification, but the result is that RSC, running TensorFloat-32 math mode, can get more FLOP\/s (floating point operations per second) per core than other, more precision-oriented systems. In this case it&#8217;s up to 1,895,000 teraFLOP\/s, or 1.9 exaFLOP\/s, more than 4x Fugaku&#8217;s. Does that matter? And if so, to whom? If anyone, it might matter to the Top 500 folks, so I&#8217;ve asked if they have any input on it. But it doesn&#8217;t change the fact that RSC will be among the fastest computers in the world, perhaps the fastest to be operated by a private company for its own purposes.<\/p><div class=\"piano-inline-promo\"><\/div>\n","protected":false},"excerpt":{"rendered":"<p>There&#8217;s a global competition to build the biggest, most powerful computers on the planet, and Meta (AKA Facebook) is about to jump into the melee with the &#8220;AI Research SuperCluster,&#8221; or RSC. Once fully operational, it may well sit in the top 10 fastest supercomputers in the world, which it will use for the massive elf":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags\/276824"}],"collection":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags"}],"about":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/taxonomies\/post_tag"}],"wp:post_type":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/posts?tags=276824"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/battlefield-companies?tags=276824"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc-media-gallery?tags=276824"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc_video?tags=276824"}],"curies":[{"name":"wp","href":"https:\/\/api.w.org\/{rel}","templated":true}]}}],[],[],[],[]]}},{"id":2260800,"date":"2022-01-21T12:18:48","date_gmt":"2022-01-21T20:18:48","guid":{"rendered":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/?p=2260800"},"modified":"2022-01-21T16:54:57","modified_gmt":"2022-01-22T00:54:57","slug":"fcc-proposal-would-curb-isp-lock-ins-at-apartment-complexes","status":"publish","type":"post","link":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/01\/21\/fcc-proposal-would-curb-isp-lock-ins-at-apartment-complexes\/","title":{"rendered":"FCC proposal would curb ISP lock-ins at apartment complexes"},"content":{"rendered":"<p id=\"speakable-summary\">Most anyone who&#8217;s moved to a new apartment has had the same experience: Whatever broadband provider everyone else in the building has, you have. This kind of lock-in could become less common if <a href=\"https:\/\/www.fcc.gov\/document\/chairwoman-rosenworcel-aims-promote-broadband-choice-apartments\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">a new proposal from the FCC<\/a> is adopted, preventing revenue-sharing between providers and building owners and opening the door to competitors.<\/p>\n<p>It&#8217;s largely because of the cost of wiring a building that an address tends to be served by a single broadband provider. And while investments should be rewarded to a certain extent, ISPs have found ways to keep strangleholds on whole neighborhoods.<\/p>\n<p>Sometimes they&#8217;re (technically) above-board, like an agreement with a big apartment complex where the building management gets kickbacks. And sometimes they&#8217;re loopholes in existing rules, where an ISP arranges things so that it would be prohibitively expensive for a competitor to share infrastructure.<\/p><div id=\"tc-target-mid-article-2260800\" class=\"tc-target-mid-article ad-unit--size-auto\"><\/div>\n<p>The new proposal, circulated internally today by FCC Chair Jessica Rosenworcel but summarized in a release, would prevent or mitigate some of these dirty tricks. It would ban revenue sharing agreements outright, require other arrangements (like marketing) to be disclosed to tenants and close loopholes that allow wiring to be effectively exclusive when it is meant to be leased or shared.<\/p>\n<p>&#8220;With more than one-third of the U.S. population living in apartments, mobile home parks, condominiums, and public housing, it\u2019s time to crack down on practices that lock out broadband competition and consumer choice,&#8221; said Rosenworcel. &#8220;Consumers deserve access to a choice of providers in their buildings. I look forward to having my colleagues join me in lifting the obstacles to competitive choice for broadband for the millions of tenants across the nation.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p>The Commission recently began looking into the issue, <a href=\"https:\/\/www.fcc.gov\/document\/fcc-seeks-comment-broadband-access-multi-tenant-buildings-0\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">opening a comment period<\/a> and investigating the evidence \u2014 not surprisingly, they found &#8220;a pattern of new practices that inhibit competition, contrary to the Commission\u2019s goals, and limit opportunities for competitive providers to offer service for apartment, condo and office building unit tenants.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p>Rosenworcel&#8217;s current proposal is the result of t1his \u2014 not a sweeping new rule but a bit of problem solving aimed squarely at bad faith activity commonly undertaken by ISPs. The proposal will be made public at some point ahead of the FCC&#8217;s vote \u2014 and even if that was tomorrow, there would likely be a considerable grace period before any of these shady arrangements are revised. So if you&#8217;re hoping to switch providers soon somewhere you&#8217;ve got no choice&#8230; keep hoping. But maybe next year.<\/p>\n","protected":false},"excerpt":{"rendered":"<p>Most anyone who&#8217;s moved to a new apartment has had the same experience: Whatever broadband provider everyone else in the building has, you have. This kind of lock-in could become less common if a new proposal from the FCC is adopted, preventing revenue-sharing between providers and building owners and opening the door to competitors. It&#8217;s researchers build an AI that learns equally well from visual, written or spoken materials"},"content":{"rendered":"<p id=\"speakable-summary\">Advances in the AI realm are constantly coming out, but they tend to be limited to a single domain: For instance, a cool new method for producing synthetic speech isn&#8217;t <em>also<\/em> a way to recognize expressions on human faces. Meta (AKA Facebook) researchers are working on something a little more versatile: an AI that can learn capably on its own whether it does so in spoken, written or visual materials.<\/p>\n<p>The traditional way of training an AI model to correctly interpret something is to give it lots and lots (like millions) of labeled examples. A picture of a cat with the cat part labeled, a conversation with the speakers and words transcribed, etc. But that approach is no longer in vogue as researchers found that it was no longer feasible to manually create databases of the sizes needed to train next-gen AIs. Who wants to label 50 million cat pictures? Okay, a few people probably \u2014 but who wants to label 50 million pictures of common fruits and vegetables?<\/p>\n<p>Currently some of the most promising AI systems are what are called self-supervised: models that can work from large quantities of unlabeled data, like books or video of people interacting, and build their own structured understanding of what the rules are of the system. For instance, by reading a thousand books it will learn the relative positions of words and ideas about grammatical structure without anyone telling it what objects or articles or commas are \u2014 it got it by drawing inferences from lots of examples.<\/p><div id=\"tc-target-mid-article-2259820\" class=\"tc-target-mid-article ad-unit--size-auto\"><\/div>\n<p>This feels intuitively more like how people learn, which is part of why researchers like it. But the models still tend to be single-modal, and all the work you do to set up a semi-supervised learning system for speech recognition won&#8217;t apply at all to image analysis \u2014 they&#8217;re simply too different. That&#8217;s where Facebook\/Meta&#8217;s latest research,<a href=\"https:\/\/ai.facebook.com\/research\/data2vec-a-general-framework-for-self-supervised-learning-in-speech-vision-and-language\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"> the catchily named data2vec<\/a>, comes in.<\/p>\n<p>The idea for data2vec was to build an AI framework that would learn in a more abstract way, meaning that starting from scratch, you could give it books to read or images to scan or speech to sound out, and after a bit of training it would learn any of those things. It&#8217;s a bit like starting with a single seed, but depending on what plant food you give it, it grows into an daffodil, pansy or tulip.<\/p>\n<p>Testing data2vec after letting it train on various data <del>corpi<\/del> corpora showed that it was competitive with and even outperformed similarly sized dedicated models for that modality. (That is to say, if the models are all limited to being 100 megabytes, data2vec did better \u2014 specialized models would probably still outperform it as they grow.)<\/p>\n<div class=\"embed breakout\">\n<blockquote class=\"wp-embedded-content\" data-secret=\"lSkDlkRHRU\"><p><a href=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/06\/09\/decades-old-ascii-adventure-nethack-may-hint-at-the-future-of-ai\/\">Decades-old ASCII adventure NetHack may hint at the future of AI<\/a><\/p><\/blockquote>\n<p><iframe class=\"wp-embedded-content\" sandbox=\"allow-scripts\" security=\"restricted\" style=\"position: absolute; clip: rect(1px, 1px, 1px, 1px);\" title=\"&#8220;Decades-old ASCII adventure NetHack may hint at the future of AI&#8221; &#8212; TechCrunch\" src=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/06\/09\/decades-old-ascii-adventure-nethack-may-hint-at-the-future-of-ai\/embed\/#?secret=lSkDlkRHRU\" data-secret=\"lSkDlkRHRU\" width=\"800\" height=\"450\" frameborder=\"0\" marginwidth=\"0\" marginheight=\"0\" scrolling=\"no\"><\/iframe><\/div>\n<p>&#8220;The core idea of this approach is to learn more generally: AI should be able to learn to do many different tasks, including those that are entirely unfamiliar,&#8221; <a href=\"https:\/\/ai.facebook.com\/blog\/the-first-high-performance-self-supervised-algorithm-that-works-for-speech-vision-and-text\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">wrote the team in a blog post<\/a>. &#8220;We also hope data2vec will bring us closer to a world where computers need very little labeled data in order to accomplish tasks.&#8221;<\/p><div class=\"piano-inline-promo\"><\/div>\n<p>&#8220;People experience the world through a combination of sight, sound and words, and systems like this could one day understand the world the way we do,&#8221; commented CEO Mark Zuckerberg on the research.<\/p>\n<p>This is still early stage research, so don&#8217;t expect the fabled &#8220;general AI&#8221; to emerge all of a sudden \u2014 but having an AI that has a generalized learning structure that works with a variety of domains and data types seems like a better, more elegant solution than the fragmented set of micro-intelligences we get by with today.<\/p>\n<p>The code for data2vec is open source; <a href=\"https:\/\/github.com\/pytorch\/fairseq\/tree\/main\/examples\/data2vec\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">it and some pretrained models are available here<\/a>.<\/p>\n","protected":false},"excerpt":{"rendered":"<p>Advances in the AI realm are constantly coming out, but they tend to be limited to a single domain: For instance, a cool new method for producing synthetic speech isn&#8217;t also a way to recognize expressions on human faces. Meta (AKA Facebook) researchers are working on something a little more versatile: an AI that can },{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/battlefield-companies?tags=81819"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc-media-gallery?tags=81819"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc_video?tags=81819"}],"curies":[{"name":"wp","href":"https:\/\/api.w.org\/{rel}","templated":true}]}},{"id":40978,"link":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/tag\/machine-learning\/","name":"machine learning","slug":"machine-learning","taxonomy":"post_tag","_links":{"self":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags\/40978"}],"collection":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags"}],"about":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/taxonomies\/post_tag"}],"wp:post_type":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/posts?tags=40978"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/battlefield-companies?tags=40978"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc-media-gallery?tags=40978"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc_video?tags=40978"}],"curies":[{"name":"wp","href":"https:\/\/api.w.org\/{rel}","templated":true}]}},{"id":171,"link":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/tag\/meta\/","name":"meta","slug":"meta","taxonomy":"post_tag","_links":{"self":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags\/171"}],"collection":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags"}],"about":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/taxonomies\/post_tag"}],"wp:post_type":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/posts?tags=171"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/battlefield-companies?tags=171"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc-media-gallery?tags=171"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc_video?tags=171"}],"curies":[{"name":"wp","href":"https:\/\/api.w.org\/{rel}","templated":true}]}}],[],[],[],[]]}},{"id":2259538,"date":"2022-01-20T09:01:46","date_gmt":"2022-01-20T17:01:46","guid":{"rendered":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/?p=2259538"},"modified":"2022-01-20T12:28:36","modified_gmt":"2022-01-20T20:28:36","slug":"metalenz-polareyes-upgrades-digital-sensing-with-polarized-light","status":"publish","type":"post","link":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/01\/20\/metalenz-polareyes-upgrades-digital-sensing-with-polarized-light\/","title":{"rendered":"Metalenz PolarEyes upgrades digital sensing with polarized light"},"content":{"rendered":"<p id=\"speakable-summary\">Tech sees differently, and can fuse multiple types of data we can&#8217;t even perceive: lidar, IR, ultrasonic and so on. <a href=\"https:\/\/www.metalenz.com\/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Metalenz<\/a>, maker of highly compact &#8220;2D&#8221; cameras for advanced sensing, hopes to bring polarized light into the mix for security and safety with its PolarEyes tech.<\/p>\n<p>Polarization isn&#8217;t a quality of light that&#8217;s often paid much attention. It has to do with the orientation of the photon&#8217;s movement as it waves its way through the air, and generally you can get the info you need from light without checking its polarization. But that doesn&#8217;t mean it&#8217;s useless.<\/p>\n<p>&#8220;Polarization generally gets thrown out, but it really can tell you something about what the objects you&#8217;re looking at are made out of. And it can find contrast that normal cameras can&#8217;t see,&#8221; said Metalenz co-founder and CEO Rob Devlin. &#8220;In healthcare, it&#8217;s been used historically to tell whether a cell is cancerous or not \u2014 the color and intensity don&#8217;t change in the visible light, but looking at polarization it works.&#8221;<\/p><div id=\"tc-target-mid-article-2259538\" class=\"tc-target-mid-article ad-unit--size-auto\"><\/div>\n<p>But polarized light cameras are pretty much only found in medical or industrial settings where their specific qualities are needed, and therefore the devices that do it are fantastically expensive and rather large. Not the kind of thing you would want clipped to the top of your laptop screen, even if you could afford the six-figure price.<\/p>\n<div class=\"embed breakout\">\n<blockquote class=\"wp-embedded-content\" data-secret=\"l3gHD1WTVk\"><p><a href=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/02\/04\/metalenz-reimagines-the-camera-in-2d-and-raises-10m-to-ship-it\/\">Metalenz reimagines the camera in 2D and raises $10M to ship it<\/a><\/p><\/blockquote>\n<p><iframe class=\"wp-embedded-content\" sandbox=\"allow-scripts\" security=\"restricted\" style=\"position: absolute; clip: rect(1px, 1px, 1px, 1px);\" title=\"&#8220;Metalenz reimagines the camera in 2D and raises $10M to ship it&#8221; &#8212; TechCrunch\" src=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/02\/04\/metalenz-reimagines-the-camera-in-2d-and-raises-10m-to-ship-it\/embed\/#?secret=l3gHD1WTVk\" data-secret=\"l3gHD1WTVk\" width=\"800\" height=\"450\" frameborder=\"0\" marginwidth=\"0\" marginheight=\"0\" scrolling=\"no\"><\/iframe><\/div>\n<p>The advance Metalenz made <a href=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/02\/04\/metalenz-reimagines-the-camera-in-2d-and-raises-10m-to-ship-it\/\">when I wrote about them last year<\/a> was reliably and inexpensively manufacturing the complex micro-scale 3D optical features to make a tiny but effective camera on a chip. These devices, Devlin said, are currently coming to market as part of an industrial 3D sensing module, partly in partnership with STMicroelectronics. But the polarization thing has more consumer-relevant applications.<\/p>\n<p>&#8220;Polarization in facial recognition tells you whether you&#8217;re looking at real human skin, or a silicone mask, or a high-quality photo or something. In automotive settings, you can detect black ice, it&#8217;s really difficult with normal cameras but it jumps out with polarization,&#8221; said Devlin.<\/p>\n<p>In the case of facial recognition, the unit could be small enough to sit alongside a normal camera in a front-facing array, like the lidar unit in iPhones that currently scans the face using tiny lasers. A polarized light sensor would instead (in this example) split the image into four, presumably corresponding to four different axes of polarization, each of which shows a slightly different version of the image. These differences can be evaluated the way the differences between images taken a small distance or time apart can, allowing the geometry and details of the face to be observed.<\/p>\n<p><div id=\"attachment_2259635\" style=\"width: 1034px\" class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/polar2-1.gif\"><img aria-describedby=\"caption-attachment-2259635\" loading=\"lazy\" class=\"size-full wp-image-2259635\" src=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/polar2-1.gif\" alt=\"Polarized light is split into 4 streams, showing different details of a face.\" width=\"1024\" height=\"844\" \/><\/a><p id=\"caption-attachment-2259635\" class=\"wp-caption-text\"><strong>Image Credits:<\/strong> Metalenz<\/p><\/div><\/p>\n<p>Polarized light has the advantage of also being able to tell the difference between materials: skin reflects light differently than a realistic mask or photo. Perhaps this isn&#8217;t a common threat in your everyday life, but if a phone manufacturer could get the same &#8220;Face ID&#8221; type feature, with added anti-spoofing security, and use something less exotic than a tiny lidar unit, they&#8217;d probably jump on the opportunity. (And Metalenz is talking to the right people here.)<\/p>\n<p>The automotive and industrial side is also useful, as telling what a given pixel you&#8217;re looking at is made of is a surprisingly complex question that usually involves identifying the object it&#8217;s part of. But using polarization data you can tell the difference between lots of materials instantly \u2014 and in fact this is part of the value proposition of <a href=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/12\/30\/voyant-raises-15m-to-scale-production-of-its-tiny-inexpensive-lidar-tech\/\">Voyant&#8217;s new lidar<\/a>. You don&#8217;t even need a lot of resolution &#8212; one polarized pixel for every hundred normal ones would still offer huge insight on a given scene.<\/p>\n<p><div id=\"attachment_2259746\" style=\"width: 1034px\" class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/PolarID-demo.jpg\"><img aria-describedby=\"caption-attachment-2259746\" loading=\"lazy\" class=\"size-full wp-image-2259746\" src=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/PolarID-demo.jpg\" alt=\"Demo of PolarID, a facial recognition system using polarized light instead of 3D sensing.\" width=\"1024\" height=\"837\" srcset=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/PolarID-demo.jpg 1662w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/PolarID-demo.jpg?resize=150,123 150w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/PolarID-demo.jpg?resize=300,245 300w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/PolarID-demo.jpg?resize=768,628 768w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/PolarID-demo.jpg?resize=680,556 680w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/PolarID-demo.jpg?resize=1536,1255 1536w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/PolarID-demo.jpg?resize=50,41 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px\" \/><\/a><p id=\"caption-attachment-2259746\" class=\"wp-caption-text\"><strong>Image Credits:<\/strong> Metalenz<\/p><\/div><\/p>\n<p>All this depends on the ability of Metalenz to make the polarized camera units small and sensitive enough to use in these situations. They&#8217;ve reduced the breadbox-scale units used industrially to a cracker-sized one they&#8217;ve been testing with, and are working on a Skittle-sized camera stack that could be added or swapped in for other camera units in robots, cars, laptops, perhaps even phones. It&#8217;s firmly in the &#8220;development&#8221; phase of research and development.<\/p>\n<p>Metalenz is currently working off last year&#8217;s A round from 3M, Applied Ventures, Intel, TDK and others, the type of crowd you expect to invest in a potentially lucrative new component type. If interest in PolarEyes is anything like what the company had for its first sensor, we can expect another raise to cover the scaling costs soon.<\/p>\n","protected":false},"excerpt":{"rendered":"<p>Tech sees differently, and can fuse multiple types of data we can&#8217;t even perceive: lidar, IR, ultrasonic and so on. Metalenz, maker of highly compact &#8220;2D&#8221; cameras for advanced sensing, hopes to bring polarized light into the mix for security and safety with its PolarEyes tech. Polarization isn&#8217;t a quality of light that&#8217;s often paid aims to provide AI-powered building security, minus bias and privacy pitfalls"},"content":{"rendered":"<p id=\"speakable-summary\">Security \u2014 as in &#8220;hey you, you can&#8217;t go in there&#8221; \u2014 quickly becomes a complex, arguably impossible job once you get past a few buildings and cameras. Who can watch everywhere at once, and send someone in time to prevent a problem? <a href=\"https:\/\/ambient.ai\/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Ambient.ai<\/a> isn&#8217;t the first to claim that AI can, but they may be the first to actually pull it off at scale \u2014 and they&#8217;ve raised $52 million to keep growing.<\/p>\n<p>The problem with today&#8217;s processes is the sort of thing anybody can point out. If you&#8217;ve got a modern company or school campus with dozens or hundreds of cameras, they produce so much footage and data that even a dedicated security team will have trouble keeping up with it. As a consequence, not only are they likely to miss an important event as it happens, but they&#8217;re also up to their ears in false alarms and noise.<\/p>\n<p>&#8220;Victims are always looking at the cameras, expecting that someone is coming to help them&#8230; but it just isn&#8217;t the case,&#8221; CEO and co-founder of Ambient.ai, Shikhar Shrestha, told TechCrunch. &#8220;Best case is you wait for the incident to happen, you go and pull the video, and you work from there. We have the cameras, we have the sensors, we have the officers \u2014 what&#8217;s missing is the brain in the middle.&#8221;<\/p><div id=\"tc-target-mid-article-2259148\" class=\"tc-target-mid-article ad-unit--size-auto\"><\/div>\n<p>Obviously Shrestha&#8217;s company is looking to provide the brain: a central visual processing unit for live security footage that can tell when something&#8217;s going wrong and immediately tell the right people. But without the bias that threatens such endeavors, and no facial recognition.<\/p>\n<p>Others have made inroads on this particular idea before now, but so far none has seen serious adoption. The first generation of automatic image recognition, Shrestha said, was simple motion detection, little more than checking whether pixels were moving around on the screen \u2014 with no insight into whether it was a tree or a home invader. Next came the use of deep learning to do object recognition: identifying a gun in hand or a breaking window. This proved useful but limited and somewhat high maintenance, needing lots of scene- and object-specific training.<\/p>\n<p>&#8220;The insight was, if you look at what humans do to understand a video, we take lots of other information: is the person sitting or standing? Are they opening a door, are they walking or running? Are they indoors or outdoors, daytime or nighttime? We bring all that together to create a kind of comprehensive understanding of the scene,&#8221; Shrestha explained. &#8220;We use computer vision intelligence to mine the footage for a whole range of events. We break down every task and call it a primitive: interactions, objects, etc., then we combine those building blocks to create a &#8216;signature.'&#8221;<\/p>\n<p><div id=\"attachment_2259192\" style=\"width: 1034px\" class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/ambientai-shot.png\"><img aria-describedby=\"caption-attachment-2259192\" loading=\"lazy\" class=\"size-full wp-image-2259192\" src=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/ambientai-shot.png\" alt=\"\" width=\"1024\" height=\"728\" srcset=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/ambientai-shot.png 1440w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/ambientai-shot.png?resize=150,107 150w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/ambientai-shot.png?resize=300,213 300w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/ambientai-shot.png?resize=768,546 768w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/ambientai-shot.png?resize=680,484 680w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/ambientai-shot.png?resize=50,36 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px\" \/><\/a><p id=\"caption-attachment-2259192\" class=\"wp-caption-text\">The Ambient.ai system uses elements of behavior and plugs them into each other to tell whether they&#8217;re a problem. <strong>Image Credits:<\/strong> Ambient.ai<\/p><\/div><\/p>\n<p>A signature may be something like &#8220;a person sitting in their car for a long time at night,&#8221; or &#8220;a person standing by a security checkpoint not interacting with anyone,&#8221; or any number of things. Some have been tweaked and added by the team, some have been arrived at independently by the model, which Shrestha descrbed as &#8220;kind of a managed semi-supervised approach.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p>The benefit of using an AI to monitor a hundred video streams at once is plain even if you were to assume that said AI is only, say, 80% as good as a human at spotting something bad happening. With no such shortcomings as distraction, fatigue or only having two eyes, an AI can apply that level of success without limits on time or feed number, meaning the chance of success is actually quite high.<\/p>\n<p>But the same might have been said of a proto-AI system from a few years ago that was only looking for guns. What Ambient.ai is aiming for is something more comprehensive.<\/p>\n<p>&#8220;We built the platform around the idea of privacy by design,&#8221; Shrestha said. With AI-powered security, &#8220;people just assume facial recognition is part of it, but with our approach you have this large number of signature events, and you can have a risk indicator without having to do facial recognition. You don&#8217;t just have one image and one model that says what&#8217;s happening &#8212; we have all these different blocks that allow you to get more descriptive in the system.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p>Essentially this is done by keeping each individual recognized activity bias-free to begin with. For instance, whether someone is sitting or standing, or how long they&#8217;ve been waiting outside a door \u2014 if each of these behaviors can be audited and found to be detected across demographics and groups, then the sum of such inferences must likewise be free of bias. In this way the system structurally reduces bias.<\/p>\n<p>It must, however, be said that bias is insidious and complex, and our ability to recognize it and mitigate it lags behind the state of the art. Nevertheless it seems intuitively true that, as Shrestha put it, &#8220;if you don&#8217;t have an inference category for something that can be biased, there&#8217;s no way for bias to come in that way.&#8221; Let&#8217;s hope so!<\/p>\n<p><div id=\"attachment_2259193\" style=\"width: 1034px\" class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/ambientai-Founders.jpg\"><img aria-describedby=\"caption-attachment-2259193\" loading=\"lazy\" class=\"size-full wp-image-2259193\" src=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/ambientai-Founders.jpg\" alt=\"Ambient.ai co-founders Vikesh Khanna (CTO, left) and Shikhar Shrestha (CEO).\" width=\"1024\" height=\"683\" srcset=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/ambientai-Founders.jpg 2880w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/ambientai-Founders.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/ambientai-Founders.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/ambientai-Founders.jpg?resize=768,512 768w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/ambientai-Founders.jpg?resize=680,453 680w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/ambientai-Founders.jpg?resize=1536,1024 1536w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/ambientai-Founders.jpg?resize=2048,1365 2048w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/ambientai-Founders.jpg?resize=50,33 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px\" \/><\/a><p id=\"caption-attachment-2259193\" class=\"wp-caption-text\">Ambient.ai co-founders Vikesh Khanna (CTO, left) and Shikhar Shrestha (CEO). <strong>Image Credits:<\/strong> Ambient.ai<\/p><\/div><\/p><div class=\"piano-inline-promo\"><\/div>\n<p>We&#8217;ve seen a few startups come and go along these lines, so it&#8217;s important for these ideas to be demonstrated on the record. And despite keeping relatively quiet about itself, Ambient.ai has a number of active customers that have helped prove out its product hypothesis. Of course the last couple years haven&#8217;t exactly been business as usual&#8230; but it&#8217;s hard to imagine &#8220;five of the largest U.S. tech companies by market cap&#8221; would be customers (and they are) if it didn&#8217;t work.<\/p>\n<p>One test at an unnamed &#8220;Fortune 500 Technology Company&#8221; was looking to reduce &#8220;tailgating,&#8221; where someone enters a secured area right behind another person authorized to do so. If you think no one does this, well, they identified 2,000 incidents the first week. But by sending GIFs of the events in near real time to security, who presumably went and wagged their fingers at the offenders, that number was reduced to 200 a week. Now it&#8217;s 10 a week, probably by people like me.<\/p>\n<p><div id=\"attachment_2259191\" style=\"width: 1034px\" class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/problems-ambientai.jpg\"><img aria-describedby=\"caption-attachment-2259191\" loading=\"lazy\" class=\"size-full wp-image-2259191\" src=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/problems-ambientai.jpg\" alt=\"Illustration of ambient.ai's interface, showing detected events like falls and active shooters.\" width=\"1024\" height=\"728\" srcset=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/problems-ambientai.jpg 1440w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/problems-ambientai.jpg?resize=150,107 150w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/problems-ambientai.jpg?resize=300,213 300w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/problems-ambientai.jpg?resize=768,546 768w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/problems-ambientai.jpg?resize=680,484 680w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/problems-ambientai.jpg?resize=50,36 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px\" \/><\/a><p id=\"caption-attachment-2259191\" class=\"wp-caption-text\"><strong>Image Credits:<\/strong> Ambient.ai<\/p><\/div><\/p>\n<p>In another test case Ambient.ai documented, a school&#8217;s security cameras caught someone scaling the fence after hours. The security head was sent the footage immediately and called the cops. Turns out the guy had priors. The point I take here is not that we need to lock down our school campuses, and this will help do that, but something else mentioned in the document, which is that the system can combine the knowledge of &#8220;someone is climbing a fence&#8221; with other stuff, like &#8220;this happens a lot a bit before 8:45,&#8221; so kids taking a shortcut don&#8217;t get the cops called on them. And the AI could also discern between climbing, falling and loitering, which in different circumstances might matter, or not.<\/p>\n<p>Ambient.ai claims that part of the system&#8217;s flexibility is in all these &#8220;primitives&#8221; being easy to rearrange depending on the needs of the site \u2014 maybe you truly don&#8217;t care if someone climbs a fence, unless they fall \u2014 as well as being able to learn new situations: &#8220;Ah, so this is what it looks like when someone is cutting a fence.&#8221; The team currently has about 100 suspicious behavior &#8220;signatures&#8221; and hopes to double that over the next year.<\/p>\n<p>Making the existing security personnel more effective by giving them more control over what blows up their phone or radio saves time and improves outcomes (Ambient.ai says it reduces the number of common alarms in general by 85-90%). And AI-powered categorization of footage helps with records and archives as well. Saying &#8220;download all footage of people climbing a fence at night&#8221; is a lot easier than scrubbing through 5,000 hours manually.<\/p>\n<p>The $52 million round was led by a16z, but there&#8217;s also a bit of a who&#8217;s-who in the individual investor pile: Ron Conway, Ali Rowghani from Y Combinator, Okta co-founder Frederic Kerrest, CrowdStrike CEO George Kurtz, Microsoft CVP Charles Dietrich, and several others who know of what they invest in.<\/p>\n<p>&#8220;It&#8217;s a unique time; security practitioners are expected to do a lot more. The basic proposal of not having to have someone watching all these feeds is universal,&#8221; said Shrestha. &#8220;We spend so much money on security, $120 billion&#8230; it&#8217;s crazy that the outcomes aren&#8217;t there \u2014 we don&#8217;t prevent incidents. It feels like all roads are leading to convergence. We want to be a platform that an organization can adopt and future-proof their security.&#8221;<\/p>\n","protected":false},"excerpt":{"rendered":"<p>Security \u2014 as in &#8220;hey you, you can&#8217;t go in there&#8221; \u2014 quickly becomes a complex, arguably impossible job once you get past a few buildings and cameras. Who can watch everywhere at once, and send someone in time to prevent a problem? Ambient.ai isn&#8217;t the first to claim that AI can, but they may 21"}],"collection":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/media"}],"about":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/types\/attachment"}],"replies":[{"embeddable":true,"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/comments?post=2259221"}],"author":[{"embeddable":true,"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/tc\/v1\/users\/12084691"}]}}],"wp:term":[[{"id":424613844,"link":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/artificial-intelligence-2\/","name":"Artificial security","slug":"physical-security","taxonomy":"post_tag","_links":{"self":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags\/224855"}],"collection":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags"}],"about":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/taxonomies\/post_tag"}],"wp:post_type":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/posts?tags=224855"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/battlefield-companies?tags=224855"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc-media-gallery?tags=224855"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc_video?tags=224855"}],"curies":[{"name":"wp","href":"https:\/\/api.w.org\/{rel}","templated":true}]}},{"id":801,"link":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/tag\/security\/","name":"security","slug":"security","taxonomy":"post_tag","_links":{"self":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags\/801"}],"collection":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags"}],"about":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/taxonomies\/post_tag"}],"wp:post_type":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/posts?tags=801"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/battlefield-companies?tags=801"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc-media-gallery?tags=801"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc_video?tags=801"}],"curies":[{"name":"wp","href":"https:\/\/api.w.org\/{rel}","templated":true}]}},{"id":449557042,"link":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/tag\/startups\/","name":"Startups","slug":"startups","taxonomy":"post_tag","_links":{"self":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags\/449557042"}],"collection":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags"}],"about":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/taxonomies\/post_tag"}],"wp:post_type":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/posts?tags=449557042"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/battlefield-companies?tags=449557042"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc-media-gallery?tags=449557042"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc_video?tags=449557042"}],"curies":[{"name":"wp","href":"https:\/\/api.w.org\/{rel}","templated":true}]}}],[],[],[],[]]}},{"id":2258923,"date":"2022-01-18T13:49:58","date_gmt":"2022-01-18T21:49:58","guid":{"rendered":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/?p=2258923"},"modified":"2022-01-18T15:51:24","modified_gmt":"2022-01-18T23:51:24","slug":"ftc-reviews-merger-guidelines-for-big-tech","status":"publish","type":"post","link":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/01\/18\/ftc-reviews-merger-guidelines-for-big-tech\/","title":{"rendered":"FTC challenges consolidation in tech with review of merger guidelines"},"content":{"rendered":"<p id=\"speakable-summary\">It&#8217;s a sign of the times that on the very same morning, the FTC and Justice Department <a href=\"https:\/\/www.ftc.gov\/news-events\/press-releases\/2022\/01\/ftc-and-justice-department-seek-to-strengthen-enforcement-against-illegal-mergers\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">announced a thorough review of corporate merger policies<\/a> with an eye to preventing anti-competitive acquisitions \u2014 and a megacorp announced a $69 billion deal that would further consolidate an industry already dominated by a handful of players.<\/p>\n<p>That&#8217;s not to say that <a href=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/01\/18\/microsoft-acquire-activision-blizzard\/\">the Microsoft-Activision deal<\/a> is necessarily anticompetitive, but they certainly will have their job cut out for them in proving that it isn&#8217;t. As a publisher, platform, hardware maker and service provider in gaming, Microsoft&#8217;s acquisition of Activision Blizzard puts several of the world&#8217;s most popular gaming franchises firmly in its stable \u2014 and potentially out of arch-rival Sony&#8217;s.<\/p>\n<p>Maybe that&#8217;s just how competition works these days. But what this FTC review presupposes is&#8230; maybe it shouldn&#8217;t?<\/p><div id=\"tc-target-mid-article-2258923\" class=\"tc-target-mid-article ad-unit--size-auto\"><\/div>\n<p>What exactly the new rules are to be isn&#8217;t yet set, the announcement drew very obvious dotted lines around the areas the agencies are hoping to rein in without actually naming names. In fact (as is frequently but not always the process in recasting rules) public comment is called for on all matters, so the areas of focus are ostensibly notional, though one assumes they have a draft ready now. (In fact I suspect FTC Chair Lina Khan had it in her briefcase when she was confirmed.)<\/p>\n<p>The review would seek to revisit the conditions under which a merger or acquisition would be viewed with suspicion, such as whether it will &#8220;tend to create a monopoly,&#8221; a somewhat loose turn of phrase technically in the guidelines now but not very well defined.<\/p>\n<div class=\"embed breakout\">\n<blockquote class=\"wp-embedded-content\" data-secret=\"4d5KDeyvdT\"><p><a href=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/06\/30\/amazon-betrays-its-fear-with-petition-to-sideline-ftc-chair-and-antitrust-hawk-lina-khan\/\">Amazon betrays its fear with petition to sideline FTC Chair and antitrust hawk Lina Khan<\/a><\/p><\/blockquote>\n<p><iframe class=\"wp-embedded-content\" sandbox=\"allow-scripts\" security=\"restricted\" style=\"position: absolute; clip: rect(1px, 1px, 1px, 1px);\" title=\"&#8220;Amazon betrays its fear with petition to sideline FTC Chair and antitrust hawk Lina Khan&#8221; &#8212; TechCrunch\" src=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/06\/30\/amazon-betrays-its-fear-with-petition-to-sideline-ftc-chair-and-antitrust-hawk-lina-khan\/embed\/#?secret=4d5KDeyvdT\" data-secret=\"4d5KDeyvdT\" width=\"800\" height=\"450\" frameborder=\"0\" marginwidth=\"0\" marginheight=\"0\" scrolling=\"no\"><\/iframe><\/div>\n<p>Also under consideration would be the way markets themselves are defined, which is key to establishing anticompetitive practices. For instance, what market is Facebook in, legally speaking? Communications, &#8220;social media&#8221; (however we define that), advertising, or what? Unless that sort of thing is pinned down, companies can slip between the cracks, arguing one day that they&#8217;re an advertiser and the next that they&#8217;re a communications provider, usually whichever suits their needs for escaping regulation. (Broadband providers have been drawing out a similar advantageous vacillation for decades.)<\/p>\n<p>The FTC will also be looking at updating guidelines on &#8220;potential and nascent competitors,&#8221; the kind frequently snapped up for tens of billions of dollars when it looks like they can&#8217;t be contained any longer.<\/p>\n<p>Monopsony power and how it relates to labor markets is also being looked at, which is where one company (or more than one in collusion) dominates the market for buying a particular service. Say if Uber for some reason employs 90% of all personal transportation drivers in a state, what opportunities for leverage and abuse does that offer, and how can those be counteracted?<\/p>\n<p>The most explicit call-out, however, is certainly the final bullet on the list of items:<\/p>\n<blockquote><p><strong>Unique characteristics of digital markets<\/strong>: The agencies seek information on how to account for key areas of the modern economy like digital markets in the guidelines, which often have characteristics like zero-price products, multi-sided markets, and data aggregation that the current guidelines do not address in detail.<\/p><\/blockquote>\n<p>What exactly is the effect of things like Amazon Prime, which despite being unquestionably convenient in many ways is part of a complex market and system of markets, and as such may prove problematic from a competition standpoint? Without the ability to even ask and answer such questions, Amazon will fall through the aforementioned cracks and its policies may be blindly accepted as beneficial upon superficial inquiry.<\/p>\n<p>Asking the general public whether they think a company amounts to a monopsony, or whether their acquisition of a competitor amounts is questionable, seems on the face of it a little unaccountable. But the public comment period does two things.<\/p><div class=\"piano-inline-promo\"><\/div>\n<p>First, it creates an opportunity for soliciting broad support from the public across the U.S., establishing the process as good politics. Some canny Representative may require a bit of convincing that their constituency cares about such things. Though as we saw with the net neutrality push, this is hardly sufficient to guarantee success.<\/p>\n<p>Second, it allows for real input from interested parties \u2014 trade associations, activists, NGOs, and so on \u2014 who can put together serious comments that make substantive policy and research suggestions. Having a basket of non-partisan think tanks to sprinkle in as footnotes justifying various approaches is a bulwark against rules being considered &#8220;arbitrary or capricious&#8221; and being nullified or challenged.<\/p>\n<p><a href=\"https:\/\/www.ftc.gov\/public-statements\/2022\/01\/statement-chair-lina-m-khan-regarding-request-information-merger\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">A highly readable statement by Khan<\/a> summarizes the situation well and gives important context to the idea of updating rules \u2014 it&#8217;s something we&#8217;ve been doing all along, and it&#8217;s long past time we do it again. It&#8217;s hard to put it better than she does, with the directness that made her Amazon&#8217;s Antitrust Paradox paper so compelling:<\/p>\n<blockquote><p>Major technological and economic changes, meanwhile, have led to shifts in how businesses compete and grow, creating new interconnections and dynamics across multiple dimensions.<\/p>\n<p>Evidence suggests that decades of mergers have been a key driver of consolidation across industries, with this latest merger wave threatening to concentrate our markets further yet.<\/p>\n<p>While the current merger boom has delivered massive fees for investment banks, evidence suggests that many Americans historically have lost out, with diminished opportunity, higher prices, lower wages, and lagging innovation. 7 A lack of competition also appears to have left segments of our economy more brittle, as consolidated supply and reduced investment in capacity can render us less resilient in the face of shocks.<\/p>\n<p>Just as we must revise our theories and models to fit new facts and evidence, we must ensure our merger guidelines accurately reflect the realities of the modern economy. Matching our analysis to contemporary business strategy requires that our tools be dynamic and holistic rather than static and atomistic.<\/p><\/blockquote>\n<p>The comments are open now and will remain so for 60 days. <a href=\"https:\/\/www.regulations.gov\/docket\/FTC-2022-0003\/document\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Feel free to add your own<\/a>.<\/p>\n<div class=\"embed breakout\">\n<blockquote class=\"wp-embedded-content\" data-secret=\"aSHXGdORyh\"><p><a href=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/12\/15\/ftc-may-consider-rule-curbing-algorithmic-discrimination-and-commercial-surveillance\/\">FTC may consider rule curbing algorithmic discrimination and &#8216;commercial surveillance&#8217;<\/a><\/p><\/blockquote>\n<p><iframe class=\"wp-embedded-content\" sandbox=\"allow-scripts\" security=\"restricted\" style=\"position: absolute; clip: rect(1px, 1px, 1px, 1px);\" title=\"&#8220;FTC may consider rule curbing algorithmic discrimination and &#8216;commercial surveillance&#8217;&#8221; &#8212; TechCrunch\" src=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/12\/15\/ftc-may-consider-rule-curbing-algorithmic-discrimination-and-commercial-surveillance\/embed\/#?secret=aSHXGdORyh\" data-secret=\"aSHXGdORyh\" width=\"800\" height=\"450\" frameborder=\"0\" marginwidth=\"0\" marginheight=\"0\" scrolling=\"no\"><\/iframe><\/div>\n","protected":false},"excerpt":{"rendered":"<p>It&#8217;s a sign of the times that on the very same morning, the FTC and Justice Department announced a thorough review of corporate merger policies with an eye to preventing anti-competitive acquisitions \u2014 and a megacorp announced a $69 billion deal that would further consolidate an industry already dominated by a handful of players. raised its prices again"},"content":{"rendered":"<p id=\"speakable-summary\">Netflix has raised the prices of its subscription services once again in the U.S. and Canada. All new subscribers will see the new prices starting today, and existing customers will receive an email notice 30 days before the increase enters effect.<\/p>\n<p>The hikes were <a href=\"https:\/\/www.reuters.com\/technology\/exclusive-netflix-raises-monthly-subscription-prices-us-canada-2022-01-14\/\">first reported by Reuters<\/a>, which Netflix told: &#8220;We\u2019re updating our prices so that we can continue to offer a wide variety of quality entertainment options.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p><a href=\"https:\/\/help.netflix.com\/en\/node\/24926\">The new prices are as follows<\/a>:<\/p><div id=\"tc-target-mid-article-2258135\" class=\"tc-target-mid-article ad-unit--size-auto\"><\/div>\n<ul>\n<li>Basic (1 stream, 480p): $8.99 \u2192 $9.99<\/li>\n<li>Standard (2 streams, 1080p): $13.99 \u2192 $15.49<\/li>\n<li>Premium (4 streams, 4K): $17.99 \u2192 $19.99<\/li>\n<\/ul>\n<p>In Canada the new prices are $16.49 for Standard and $20.99 for Premium, with Basic unchanged at $9.99.<\/p>\n<p><a href=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2020\/10\/29\/netflix-is-raising-prices-on-most-of-its-plans-in-the-us\/\">The last increase was in 2020<\/a> (standard went from $13 to $14) and <a href=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2019\/01\/15\/netflix-price-hike\/\">before that 2019<\/a> (from $11 to $13). One imagines they waited until January just so no one can say they raised the price three years in a row.<\/p>\n<p>The pandemic has seen something of a historic increase in demand for TV and movies, and while Hollywood is still trying to sort out its strategy, Netflix has confidently doubled down on a winning formula and slipped its customers the bill.<\/p>\n<p>While nobody likes to lose another buck a month, the value proposition is hard to deny with the platform&#8217;s seemingly endless selection of increasingly international productions. The breakout hit &#8220;Squid Game&#8221; is just one example of a show that probably wouldn&#8217;t have been made (indeed the creator shopped it unsuccessfully for years) without Netflix&#8217;s deep pockets.<\/p><div class=\"piano-inline-promo\"><\/div>\n<p>Expect an email soon detailing the changes \u2014 you&#8217;ll have a chance to change or cancel, but who among us has the strength to do so? Just use the increase as justification for watching 10% more shows.<\/p>\n","protected":false},"excerpt":{"rendered":"<p>Netflix has raised the prices of its subscription services once again in the U.S. and Canada. All new subscribers will see the new prices starting today, and existing customers will receive an email notice 30 days before the increase enters effect. The hikes were first reported by Reuters, which Netflix told: &#8220;We\u2019re updating our prices
